  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9d574d47-6a15-4c69-b475-c859e1dddbe3'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer8793200877854933286">A man-made plague has swept the earth, but a small group survives, along with the green-eyed Crakers – a gentle species bio-engineered to replace humans. Toby, onetime member of the Gods Gardeners and expert in mushrooms and bees, is still in love with street-smart Zeb, who has an interesting past. The Crakers’ reluctant prophet, Snowman-the-Jimmy, is hallucinating; Amanda</span>
  <span id="freeText8793200877854933286" style="display:none">A man-made plague has swept the earth, but a small group survives, along with the green-eyed Crakers – a gentle species bio-engineered to replace humans. Toby, onetime member of the Gods Gardeners and expert in mushrooms and bees, is still in love with street-smart Zeb, who has an interesting past. The Crakers’ reluctant prophet, Snowman-the-Jimmy, is hallucinating; Amanda is in shock from a Painballer attack; and Ivory Bill yearns for the provocative Swift Fox, who is flirting with Zeb. Meanwhile, giant Pigoons and malevolent Painballers threaten to attack. <br /><br />Told with wit, dizzying imagination, and dark humour, Booker Prize-winning Margaret Atwood’s unpredictable, chilling and hilarious MaddAddam takes us further into a challenging dystopian world and holds up a skewed mirror to our own possible future.</span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/25801961-leslie-fisher"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1466298779i/25801961._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/25801961-leslie-fisher">Leslie Fisher</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I found that with the first book, I wasn't sure how I felt about the series, but in the end, I was glad I read it. I think it made me enjoy the follow<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I found that with the first book, I wasn't sure how I felt about the series, but in the end, I was glad I read it. I think it made me enjoy the following 2 books even more. I enjoyed seeing different sides of the same situation and looking for correlations between the books.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer631135551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17361624824092582212">Oh, dear. Okay. It's only fair that I say something about this novel since karen was kindly enough to gift me the ARC so I could read it before it came out, but you should know that a large part of me doesn't even want to discuss this because like you, I went to grade school and had it drilled in my head that if you can't think of something nice to say, you shouldn't say anything at all. I have of course strayed wildly from that path as a grownup-ish humanoid, but this is Margaret Atwood we're t</span>
  <span id="freeText17361624824092582212" style="display:none">Oh, dear. Okay. It's only fair that I say something about this novel since karen was kindly enough to gift me the ARC so I could read it before it came out, but you should know that a large part of me doesn't even want to discuss this because like you, I went to grade school and had it drilled in my head that if you can't think of something nice to say, you shouldn't say anything at all. I have of course strayed wildly from that path as a grownup-ish humanoid, but this is Margaret Atwood we're talking about here. I respect her like crazy, and if you do too, then I think you deserve some answers for my blase reaction to this final installment in her MaddAddam Trilogy. <br /><br />Don't shoot the messenger. <br /><br />Naturally, I was very excited about this, both before I had it in my hands and after the first couple of minichapters. Like a True Blood episode, the story cold opens right where <i>The Year of the Flood</i> and <i>Oryx and Crake</i> ended. Our storylines have merged, and off we go, zooooom! Right back in the shit. And then...<br /><br />And then the story goes all daytime drama. I don't know how to structure each criticism or praise into a cohesive series of paragraphs considering I am a bit all over the place about this, so I'll use the ole point by point in no discernible order method.<br /><br />*Toby. Remember Toby? She's the strong, commanding female presence in <i>The Year of the Flood</i>, the one who endures horrific sexual torture, hollows out her reproductive system in order to make rent without letting it ruin her sense of self, changes her identity/appearance and gives up her very existence to go into hiding so she could protect those she loved in God's Gardeners, holes up and weathers the plague all alone by resourcefully hoarding and cultivating food and other essentials <i>while</i> fighting off hybrid monster animal things and rapists alike, the woman who without a second thought grabs a shotgun and chases after Amanda when she gets grabbed by the Painballers, at great and unnecessary risk to herself. You know, Toby: the baaaaad motherfucker. Well, in <i>MaddAddam</i>, I started getting a little convinced that Toby had been replaced by a Stepford Wife. To say she's lovesick throughout the novel is, oh boy, quite the understatement. Seriously, all she thinks about (and she is the primary narrator) is Zeb. Zeb, Zeb, Zebby Debby Doo. Zibbity Dibbity Dib Doodle Doo, I wuv you. Did the world end? Who cares when Zeb may be cheating on me? Is he cheating on me? He's talking to one of the girls. They were out hunting for a long time. Does that mean he's fucking her? Oh no, what if he's fucking her? Considering he's my lover, I should probably just ask instead of sitting here speculating for days and days and days, but that wouldn't take up 30 chapters. I'll just whine in my diary like a 13 year old instead, because apparently that's what badass Toby does now. Seriously, this Toby would have been jealous if Zeb owned a female cat. And that just did not sit well with me. I couldn't accustom myself to this new and strange desire to smack this character. She was my favorite!<br /><br />*Zeb. His story, and the way it merged with that of Adam One = best part of the book. He is the magnet pulling together all the crazy dystopian, hypertechy world elements in the first and second novels, from the oil-based religious groups and creepy child murder trade stuff and simulated, interactive torture sex and guillotine deaths of naked women via a much evolved internet, government (i.e. corporate) sponsored cannibalism, Battle Royale style live entertainment (Painball), the works. All of human nature's worst on parade, a blanket numb acceptance on its way to flagrant embracing of debauchery, exploitation, overconsumption, and violence. You know, the important stuff, the good stuff, the what was fascinating about this story stuff. Any time we were in this world (not enough, I'd argue, but still) I was back in the swing of the novel, guns blazing.<br /><br />*The writing - and never before have I said this about Atwood - felt droll in places and just sort of flat and forced in some others. The dialogue in particular irked me considering the urgent danger these characters were in of being massacred by the painballers. I know people joke around, and that these characters are of differing intelligence levels and ages with disparate priorities and senses of humor and means of dealing with stress and whatnot, but everybody sorta talked like it was all one big joke. Like it was just another lazy day on the porch swing with straw in teeth and feet propped up, just shooting the shit while watching the sun set. I admit that I've never personally been stalked by two psychopathic, cannibal rapists with crazy futuristic guns in a lawless post-apocalyptic warzone, but I don't think I would be cracking dick jokes and worrying about petty jealousies if I were. Well, maybe the dick jokes, but not all the time!<br /><br />*Jimmy. Remember Snowman? From <i>Oryx and Crake</i>? Well, if you were excited to catch back up on his story like I was, then I'm sorry to let you down, but he's in a coma for the majority of the novel. If you hated <i>Oryx and Crake</i> and find Jimmy's storyline uninteresting, then I am pleased to inform you that he is in a coma for the majority of the novel.<br /><br />*Amanda. Oy vey, that was a fascinating and powerful descent which was painfully executed to great effect in this final novel. Amanda was the tough as a tiger pleebrat from the second novel, the resourceful youth who wiggled her way into Ren's life and infiltrated the God's Gardeners even despite her strikingly contradictory personality, back-story, and beliefs. In <i>MaddAddam</i>, our other super-strong female character (meaning Amanda) has been almost completely crushed by her experiences with the painballers. She is near-catatonic, doesn't do much but slowly seap tears, and has been stripped of her fiery personality as a result of being brutalized in ways you couldn't/shouldn't even imagine. PTSD like crazy, which is both understandable and horrible given the circumstances. Any scene she was in gave me the willies. Much as I don't like what she has been through, Atwood handled that part of the story, specifically the shift in power dynamics between her and her best friend Ren which resulted, gracefully and hauntingly. I'm saddened to lose these two characters forever. And I am taping my boobs up and wearing a fake mustache <i>everywhere</i> if I ever end up at the end of the world, because Jesus Fucking Christ.<br /><br />It looks like I did end up ranting after all. Allow me to throw some qualifiers your way: my expectations were way high about this book so I could be being harsh, the ending is wonderful and ties everything up pretty nicely so you should read this for that catharsis alone, and I don't know shit about shit so you shouldn't listen to me about anything anyway. I am glad I read it. Certain things just bugged me, but that could be because this story and I have known each other for about 5 years now, and may be at the point where we are just expecting to let one another down because it's all we know anymore. Over-familiarity is a definite possibility. That doesn't mean I'm not sad to see her go. Please don't let me keep you from giving this novel a fighting chance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17361624824092582212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_706088503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/706088503" />
    <a title="Daniel Kukwa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3885089-daniel-kukwa"><img alt="Daniel Kukwa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327106553p2/3885089.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/706088503?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Kukwa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Kukwa" href="/user/show/3885089-daniel-kukwa">Daniel Kukwa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3885089-daniel-kukwa?shelf=canadian-lit">canadian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3885089-daniel-kukwa?shelf=other-sf-fantasy">other-sf-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer706088503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2992503780285794014">At first, I was disappointed. Where were the epic final confrontations? Where was the catharsis, after two novels of terrifying, complex build-up? A third of the way through, it hit me: none of that actually matters to the novel. The entire "final battle" is almost an afterthought, compared to the main themes of hope rising from the ashes, the power of love & loyalty, and the fact that human civilization adapts...spitting proudly in the eye of dystopia. This is a story about "telling" the storie</span>
  <span id="freeText2992503780285794014" style="display:none">At first, I was disappointed. Where were the epic final confrontations? Where was the catharsis, after two novels of terrifying, complex build-up? A third of the way through, it hit me: none of that actually matters to the novel. The entire "final battle" is almost an afterthought, compared to the main themes of hope rising from the ashes, the power of love &amp; loyalty, and the fact that human civilization adapts...spitting proudly in the eye of dystopia. This is a story about "telling" the stories of characters -- Zeb &amp; Toby's romance, Zeb &amp; Adam's coming of age, the Crakers &amp; their enlightenment through the young Blackbeard...and the novel is quite seductive (to say nothing of snort-inducing hilarious when it comes to dealing with profanity) as it constructs its narrative. I might not have received what I was expecting, but I certainly am satisfied with the end result. It leaves a warm glow behind, one worthy of the Crakers and their boundless optimism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2992503780285794014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_690370330" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/690370330" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2431873-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1477433724p2/2431873.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/690370330?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/2431873-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18621313-maddaddam">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2431873-emily?shelf=post-apocalyptic-dystopian">post-apocalyptic-dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer690370330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16495503364066178587">I have never been this unimpressed with a Margaret Atwood novel. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam_Trilogy__3_' title='MaddAddam (MaddAddam Trilogy #3) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>MaddAddam</a> is a tedious slog through the events of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake__MaddAddam_Trilogy___1_' title='Oryx and Crake (MaddAddam Trilogy, #1) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>Oryx and Crake</a> - <i>again</i>. While this technique worked incredibly well in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6080337.The_Year_of_the_Flood__MaddAddam_Trilogy___2_' title='The Year of the Flood (MaddAddam Trilogy, #2) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Year of the Flood</a>, providing context for much of the events and letting the female characters flip Jimmy's story on its head, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam_Trilogy__3_' title='MaddAddam (MaddAddam Trilogy #3) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>MaddAddam</a> totally fails to provide anything new or interesting in its backstory. <br/><br/>You might think that, now that all of the characters have met up after the <i>end of the world</i>, there woul</span>
  <span id="freeText16495503364066178587" style="display:none">I have never been this unimpressed with a Margaret Atwood novel. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam_Trilogy__3_" title="MaddAddam (MaddAddam Trilogy #3) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">MaddAddam</a> is a tedious slog through the events of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake__MaddAddam_Trilogy___1_" title="Oryx and Crake (MaddAddam Trilogy, #1) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Oryx and Crake</a> - <i>again</i>. While this technique worked incredibly well in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6080337.The_Year_of_the_Flood__MaddAddam_Trilogy___2_" title="The Year of the Flood (MaddAddam Trilogy, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Year of the Flood</a>, providing context for much of the events and letting the female characters flip Jimmy's story on its head, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam_Trilogy__3_" title="MaddAddam (MaddAddam Trilogy #3) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">MaddAddam</a> totally fails to provide anything new or interesting in its backstory. <br /><br />You might think that, now that all of the characters have met up after the <i>end of the world</i>, there would be some forward momentum in the present-day. You would be wrong. If you're interested in what happens to Jimmy, the erstwhile protagonist of the first novel, or any of the characters from the second novel - Toby, Ren, or Amanda - then you are going to be sorely disappointed. Toby's arc during this book is particularly terrible, but Ren and Amanda only escape this fate because they spend most of their time offscreen. Jimmy spends most of the novel in a coma. In fact, the entire plot of the present-day revolves around the Crakers and the Maddaddamites defending themselves from two rogue Painballers. It's just as boring as it sounds.<br /><br />Even if you're reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam_Trilogy__3_" title="MaddAddam (MaddAddam Trilogy #3) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">MaddAddam</a> to be further immersed in the dystopian world that Margaret Atwood created in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake__MaddAddam_Trilogy___1_" title="Oryx and Crake (MaddAddam Trilogy, #1) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Oryx and Crake</a>, you'll probably still be disappointed. 75% of this novel takes place in the past, rehashing Zeb's life in the pleeblands and his relationship with Adam One. There are a few interesting new additions to the world, but the vast majority of Zeb's activities are tied to the other books' plot lines; his story just retells what you already know. <br /><br />Finally, the writing in this book is just plain bad. There are a couple moments where the prose recalls the first two books, but they are few and far between. The metaphors have all been done before. The characters are flat and static. The Craker story sections are beyond painful. The pacing is puzzling. It's just one big mess. <br /><br />Because I can't keep it all spoiler-free, here are some things I thoroughly loathed about this novel:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[- The PLOT. Jesus Mary and Joseph, the PLOT. The only thing that happens in this book revolves around the two Painballers who violently murdered Oates in the last book and raped and assaulted Amanda. "Should we kill them? Should we chase them around?" Well, IDK guys, it sounds like you've got a lot of kudzu and fake coffee to consume, so please tell me more about that instead.<br /><br />- This is a horribly cheap resolution for Jimmy. He spends most of the book in a coma, and his only real action is seeing the bodies of Oryx and Crake - again. Why did he need to stick around for that? And he dies offscreen! <br /><br />- Toby spends this entire novel being jealous every time Zeb leaves her sight. Girl, you were a real character in the last book. I'm not sure where this iteration of you came from. The end of your arc is also terrible.<br /><br />- Who is Zeb's father? If every single person in these books can somehow be relentlessly shoehorned into Crake's orbit, then there's gotta be a way to find out who fathered Zeb.<br /><br />- Ren, Amanda, and Swift Fox all have Craker offspring. Really? REALLY. Their only function in this book is to have Craker babies? REALLY. <br /><br />- I found the Craker stories off-putting and wholly overdone. While it was interesting to read about the Crakers beginning to understand writing so soon - ha ha, Crake - I thought these sections really suffered from overuse. I would have preferred this time to be spent giving any one of the named human characters a purpose, since most of the time the names were just used to list off who was going on what expedition, but I suppose that could be a personal preference. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I don't know if it's possible to only read the first two books in this trilogy and resist reading the third. Maybe I would have appreciated this more if I had read it immediately after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6080337.The_Year_of_the_Flood__MaddAddam_Trilogy___2_" title="The Year of the Flood (MaddAddam Trilogy, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Year of the Flood</a>, but I doubt it. I really felt like this volume added absolutely nothing. There's no resolution, no catharsis ... I closed this feeling relieved that I was finally done. If you're strong enough to resist, don't read this one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16495503364066178587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10561042129069403960">I was so excited about this last book of the MaddAddam trilogy and I saw the book in Waterstones couple of weeks in hardcover. I could not resist.... and read!<br/><br/>I read the reviews and saw that some readers were somewhat disappointed about this final MaddAddam. I really don't care....   <span id="freeText10561042129069403960" style="display:none">I was so excited about this last book of the MaddAddam trilogy and I saw the book in Waterstones couple of weeks in hardcover. I could not resist.... and read!<br /><br />I read the reviews and saw that some readers were somewhat disappointed about this final MaddAddam. I really don't care.... I am amazed still by the content, imagination and cleverness of this story and this great writer. <br /><br />Yes the first book was amazing, mindboggling, what's happening here... the second book shocking and up close &amp; personal. The third book, brings it all together for me. Touching story, those people you got to know in the previous books get closer, the beautiful way of writing and decribing the scenes, it's even more personal and it's closure. How can anyone invent a story like this... Yes, there are elements in this story which were... sort of... out of place for me. Will not get into detail because then i'd spoil.... But the whole story more than made up for it. What a great story, what a great writer. Extraordinary. One of the highlights of my reading in 2013. And highly recommended. Big five star.</span>

<div id="review_1610458378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1610458378" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1610458378?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1610458378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8255016452725838659">I have completion anxiety, or whatever it's called. If it's a trilogy of books we are talking about, YES, it WILL take me YEARS to complete. Thankfully, Maddaddam book three immediately reminds you of why you fell in love with Atwood's postapocalyptic world in the first place.<br/><br/>It has parts of "Oryx and Crake" & "Year of the Flood" in it... the former a sprawling genesis of the apocalypse, the latter a more personal tale of what it takes to survive it. Maddaddam contains a mixture of both (there i</span>
  <span id="freeText8255016452725838659" style="display:none">I have completion anxiety, or whatever it's called. If it's a trilogy of books we are talking about, YES, it WILL take me YEARS to complete. Thankfully, Maddaddam book three immediately reminds you of why you fell in love with Atwood's postapocalyptic world in the first place.<br /><br />It has parts of "Oryx and Crake" &amp; "Year of the Flood" in it... the former a sprawling genesis of the apocalypse, the latter a more personal tale of what it takes to survive it. Maddaddam contains a mixture of both (there is an immediate- and an epic-like quality to it). It is sad and, actually, very very funny. This is MARGARET ATWOOD'S "FUNNIEST" novel to date. She's constructed a world that resembles this one--all the main figures (avatars of Jesus and Adam &amp; Eve, and Cain and Abel) have been represented well. They are emblematic. They are, most of them, video game-playing persons who've given life to a new world order.<br /><br />I really admire the writer's impulse to create a world larger than "The Handmaid's Tale" (her most famous book) and to have a blast creating worlds that incorporate nature &amp; technology both. <br /><br />This is (probably) one of the best literary trilogies.<br /><br />It's very satisfying, to say the least.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8255016452725838659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2762664697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2762664697" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2762664697?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781844087877" href="/book/show/20448875-maddaddam">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=reviewed-for-fantasy-book-review">reviewed-for-fantasy-book-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=2-star-reads">2-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2762664697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer628428348730674966">Some questions are best left unanswered, and some endings are better off unwritten because sometimes the question itself is what makes the piece so extraordinary. Revealing what happens next could only ever be disappointing. <br/><br/>This felt like a massive overwrite. Atwood is only joining the dots here, piecing together the threads of storytelling and character arcs left open from the previous two instalments (that were only ever vaguely related to each other.) It’s like a forced conclusion as it’s al</span>
  <span id="freeText628428348730674966" style="display:none">Some questions are best left unanswered, and some endings are better off unwritten because sometimes the question itself is what makes the piece so extraordinary. Revealing what happens next could only ever be disappointing. <br><br>This felt like a massive overwrite. Atwood is only joining the dots here, piecing together the threads of storytelling and character arcs left open from the previous two instalments (that were only ever vaguely related to each other.) It’s like a forced conclusion as it’s all shoved into a box with a nice little bow wrapped around it. It’s also terribly repetitive, going over the events from <i>Oryx and Crake</i> again. Much of this novel is stuck in the past and it adds very little to the trilogy at large. It has no momentum and no presence. <br><br>I honestly don’t understand why she even bothered to write it. There’s nothing new here. A huge part of the book is told from the perspective of Zeb (a minor character from book two) and we learn his experience about events we’ve already read about. I just don’t get it, as the book plods on at a mind numbingly boring pace nothing really happens. The ending itself (or at least the event that I saw as bringing Jimmy’s story to closure) was absolutely pointless. <br><br>And, in a way, this makes me massively worried about what’s to come next. Later this year, she is releasing a sequel to <i>The Handmaid’s Tale.</i> Part of the that book's brilliance was the openness of its ending. Atwood said all that she needed to; the particulars of the plot weren’t overly important, so I’m a little worried she may fall into a similar trap. <br><br><b>
  <u>I hope I never rate an Atwood novel this low again. </u>
</b><br><br><u>MaddAddam Trilogy</u><br>1. Oryx and Crake - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2652422790?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">5 stars</a><br>2. The Year of the Flood - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2708771268?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">5 stars</a><br>3. MaddAddam - <b>2 stars</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1553503584i/27261873._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>_________________________________<br><br>You can connect with me on social media via <a href="https://linktr.ee/sean.ultra_vegan1" rel="nofollow noopener">My Linktree</a>.<br>__________________________________</span>
  <a data-text-id="628428348730674966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2449849843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2449849843" />
    <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton"><img alt="J.L.   Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438142892p2/3675162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2449849843?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.L.   Sutton" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton">J.L.   Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2449849843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13695315671135648611"><b>“People need such stories, because however dark, a darkness with voices in it is better than a silent void.” </b><br/><br/><img alt='Image result for margaret atwood' src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583863883i/29086221._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>After enjoying the first two books in the series so well (Oryx and Crake and The Year of the Flood), I had some apprehension about Margaret Atwood's final book in the series, MaddAddam. This book takes up where both of the first two books end, and follows what is ostensibly the last remnant of humanity as we had known it (along with the genetically engineered humanoids known as Crakers).</span>
  <span id="freeText13695315671135648611" style="display:none"><b>“People need such stories, because however dark, a darkness with voices in it is better than a silent void.” </b><br><br><img alt="Image result for margaret atwood" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583863883i/29086221._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>After enjoying the first two books in the series so well (Oryx and Crake and The Year of the Flood), I had some apprehension about Margaret Atwood's final book in the series, MaddAddam. This book takes up where both of the first two books end, and follows what is ostensibly the last remnant of humanity as we had known it (along with the genetically engineered humanoids known as Crakers). I was looking forward to seeing what happens to Jimmy (the Snowman), Toby and the Crakers who are the real future of humanity. <br><br>In MaddAddam, we do learn more about Jimmy and Toby, but the Crakers become the real story. We gain a greater appreciation of the Crakers along with the Pigoons (human/pig spliced species) who had previously been depicted as intelligent but malevolent. They are both seen as being part of the natural order that will continue after humans have left the stage for good. This makes the stories that Crakers clamor for all that more important. These stories will be the basis for future generations who will likely leave their childlike innocence behind and evolve in a new world that had been prepared for them by the 'waterless flood.' This is further emphasized by the young Craker named Blackbeard who learns to write and participates in the storytelling. <br><br>This one took a while to get going (and that is my biggest complaint about MaddAddam). Even off of fresh reads of the first two books, I wasn't fully engaged until nearly halfway through. After that, it was a wild, interesting and satisfying ride to the end. 4.25 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="13695315671135648611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_976550315" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/976550315" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/976550315?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=margaret-atwood">margaret-atwood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer976550315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17208597249878327956">Ok, MaddAddam! <br/> <br/>Let me say straight away that I don't like sequels that much. But then again, I didn't like dystopian fiction at all before I read "Oryx and Crake" some 10 years ago. After "The Year of the Flood" came out 4 years ago, I found myself unable to wait for the paperback version, and I didn't even consider waiting when "MaddAddam" was announced.<br/><br/>I don't know what I expected and I think I should probably re-read the first two books to grasp all ideas in MaddAddam, but my instant feeling</span>
  <span id="freeText17208597249878327956" style="display:none">Ok, MaddAddam! <br /> <br />Let me say straight away that I don't like sequels that much. But then again, I didn't like dystopian fiction at all before I read "Oryx and Crake" some 10 years ago. After "The Year of the Flood" came out 4 years ago, I found myself unable to wait for the paperback version, and I didn't even consider waiting when "MaddAddam" was announced.<br /><br />I don't know what I expected and I think I should probably re-read the first two books to grasp all ideas in MaddAddam, but my instant feeling is that the third one is the best in the series, for a reason I didn't anticipate at all.<br /><br />The first two definitely show off more action from different angles, but MaddAddam gives the philosophical background to the story. It is amazing to watch a new civilisation emerge, including creation stories that start out as random bedtime whispers without any claim on accuracy and then gain power and consequently truth. I love the parts when the artificially created humanoid Crakers evolve from their vegetable state into something more complicated - using words, written and spoken, to build a new religion. Names gain power: You can't change Snowman-the-Jimmy to Jimmy-the-Snowman, as Toby realises when talking to the little Craker boy Blackbeard.<br /><br />This book is not about humanity gone berserk destroying our planet anymore, it is about creating a world based on our needs: food, shelter, love, sex, stories. It is about adaptation and evolution, survival of those able to find a niche - not necessarily the physically strongest of the species, but definitely the most versatile. Zeb survives multiple death threats by changing shape and function, and that is symptomatic for all characters. Survival of the adaptable, a Darwinist take on life, but not materialistic alone, since the human need for myth, ritual and love is pointed out over and over again.<br /><br />I can see why MaddAddam doesn't appeal to everyone - being a very slow and quiet read compared to the first two books - but as for me, I was more deeply satisfied with this story and would put it on one shelf with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a>.<br /><br />Having said that, you have to read the first two books to get a feeling for the strange world Toby and Zeb and the Crakers are moving in! <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake__MaddAddam___1_" title="Oryx and Crake (MaddAddam, #1) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Oryx and Crake</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6080337.The_Year_of_the_Flood__MaddAddam___2_" title="The Year of the Flood (MaddAddam, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Year of the Flood</a> are the Genesis stories of MaddAddam, and like it or not, to understand the building up of the new civilization, you have to know where it is coming from.<br /><br />Postscript: As I am currently trying to figure out whether or not I like science fiction, and why some of it still seems like utter trash, while other books make me laugh and cry - I think I have come closer to an answer: I definitely like the reflection on society and where it is headed, but I can't be bothered with the shallow adventures and fights between half-human antiheroes. there has to be a social question involved, which is why books like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/169756.Feed" title="Feed by M.T. Anderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Feed</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/924194.The_Last_Book_in_the_Universe" title="The Last Book in the Universe by Rodman Philbrick" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Book in the Universe</a> appeal to me. They point out the dangers in our society to a younger audience and open up a discussion on language, community and values. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/169756.Feed" title="Feed by M.T. Anderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Feed</a> features a press release from the government, explaining in detail that the boss didn't mean to hurt anyone when he said "Big Shithead", and that it in fact was an American idiom expressing respect. Opening the Economist this morning, I find scarily similar reports, twisting language to extreme absurdities. Margaret Atwood works with the same kind of idea. The best example in my opinion is when Snowman mutters "Oh Fuck", and the confused Crakers ask about the meaning. Being used to worship anything preceded by "Oh", they receive the explanation that "Oh Fuck" is someone you call for help when you are particularly upset. This became an instant running gag between me and a friend. "Is it time to call on the great deity yet?" we would ask. It is a book for fans of myth-making.<br /><br />This of course is a very personal reflection, and probably not at all representative.<br /><br />It's just what it is - for me! And that is why I liked MaddAddam so much while others have said with some right that it was superfluous.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17208597249878327956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_734106895" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/734106895" />
    <a title="Oriana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30800-oriana"><img alt="Oriana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549246213p2/30800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/734106895?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oriana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oriana" href="/user/show/30800-oriana">Oriana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30800-oriana?shelf=read-2013">read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30800-oriana?shelf=zeitgeist-y">zeitgeist-y</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer734106895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3922942519237925422"><b>before reading:</b> May I tell you about my borderline-psychotic quest to score an advance proof of this book?<br/><br/>It involved contacting literally every single person on GR who had reviewed this pre-publication, in order to prostrate myself and beg them to loan me their copy. Of those who dignified my crazy request with a response, a few had been given advance editions on the promise of never sharing them, and the rest had read it in e-galleys, which fuck that. And anyway, those like disappear as soon a</span>
  <span id="freeText3922942519237925422" style="display:none"><b>before reading:</b> May I tell you about my borderline-psychotic quest to score an advance proof of this book?<br /><br />It involved contacting literally every single person on GR who had reviewed this pre-publication, in order to prostrate myself and beg them to loan me their copy. Of those who dignified my crazy request with a response, a few had been given advance editions on the promise of never sharing them, and the rest had read it in e-galleys, which fuck that. And anyway, those like disappear as soon as you read them or something, right?<br /><br />So I writhed and cursed and slavered and champed at all the bits, and then I re-read both <i>Oryx and Crake</i> and <i>The Year of the Flood</i>, and then I haunted eBay every fucking day, until I got in a bidding war for a proof in Australia that almost cost me $70, and I realized I had very nearly lost my damn mind. <br /><br />So, picking myself up and dusting myself off, I decided on a distraction in the form of <i>1Q84</i>, in the actually not-insane hope that by the time I got through that monstrosity, the world would be willing to reward me with a moderately priced <i>MaddAddam</i> proof. <br /><br />And guess what? It goddamn worked. <br /><br />***<br /><br /><b>after reading:</b> Another thing that happened on my frantic quest to snatch a pre-pub copy of this book was that several people told me that I shouldn't be expending so much effort on this hunt because the book actually wasn't all that good. <br /><br />I would like to fight those people.<br /><br /><i>MaddAddam</i> is fucking brilliant, just like everything Margaret Atwood touches is fucking brilliant. <br /><br />I will admit that at first I was a little nervous. Without being too spoilery—this book is post-apocalyptic, and a new kind of human has been introduced after 99% of people died off. This race is called the Crakers, and they are about a quarter animal, in various ways, and consequently they are incredibly simplistic, lacking the understanding of the vast majority of human ways and culture. We had the Crakers in the first two books of this trilogy, as oddities primarily seen and rarely heard. But in this volume they are front and center, and I was nervous because I thought maybe Margaret was going to become a victim of her own creation—characters like this are kind of annoying, with their stilted manner of speaking, their endless questions, forcing all the regular people to constantly break everything down into its most simplistic essence to explain it to them. I thought, at the start, that it would get pretty grating to have to trudge through these kinds of exchanges for 300+ pages. <br /><br />That's why Margaret is a genius and I am but a naïf. <br /><br />She deftly maneuvers around this obstacle, defusing it in many ways—sometimes the regular human characters step in to gently mock the Crakers, giving the reader a break and an ally; sometimes watching the humans try to talk to them forces the reader herself to think about how she would explain a concept like "car" to a being essentially from another plane of existence. And sometimes, of course, the Crakers act as idiot savants, forcing the humans to confront, deconstruct, and revise their own ideas and actions. Plus, of course, they sometimes become heroes in their own right, revealing hidden depths to their seemingly placid exteriors. <br /><br />Enough about the Crakers. Another thing this book does brilliantly is to gently skewer the idea of religion, to lay bare and make plausible the way a culture defines itself by its origin story and its creation myths, showing how some of the truly preposterous tales at the heart of any religion might have once been grounded in a reality that is now too far in the past to even comprehend.<br /><br />I can't give examples. I keep trying but they get spoilery.<br /><br />Another thing is how meticulous and (harrowingly) believable Margaret Atwood's apocalypse is. Unlike the world of <i>Handmaid's Tale</i>, for example, which I don't recall feeling much connected to, this one is <i>ours</i>, completely, with current technologies, conflicts, corporate alliances, and such taken just a few steps further to their logical but deeply terrifying conclusions. I've heard that this is called "speculative fiction," which sounds about right to me. I mean, if you don't want to wander around listening to a genius speculating on how horribly awry everything we know could go, then I don't really have anything left to say to you.<br /><br />Oh, and in case anyone wants to claim that Margaret is getting old and/or out of touch, she also throws off phrases like "I did it for the lulz" perfectly, with the most casual aplomb. Because did I mention that she's a genius?<br /><br />I don't know, this review is a jumbled mess. Whatever. <br /><br />Here's the last thing I have to say: this shit made me cry at the end, in public, unapologetically, both because everything she does to her characters is terrible but also absolutely right, and also because, motherfuck, now these books are done, the whole brilliant terrible wonderful devastating marvelous thing is seriously truly over, and that is just the goddamn pits. <br /><br />The world feels a little (a lot) dimmer knowing I don't have another Atwood waiting for me just as soon as I can make my way to a copy, or force a copy to make its way to me, is what I'm trying to say.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3922942519237925422" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_565888773" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/565888773" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/927041-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418790288p2/927041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/565888773?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/927041-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/927041-jen?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/927041-jen?shelf=scifi-fantasy-dystopia">scifi-fantasy-dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer565888773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15941202698903694478">I wish I hadn't been so disappointed in this book. Why was I so disappointed in this book? I finished it Saturday night and haven't really been able to gather the brain power to assign any words to this. Yesterday I read though some of the reviews and found many of the things that bothered me articulated much more clearly by other reviewers here:<br/><br/>Badass Toby became lovesick high school girl Toby, whose entire identity seems to grow from her love interest. WTF feminist writer Margaret Atwood?<br/><br/>Jimmy</span>
  <span id="freeText15941202698903694478" style="display:none">I wish I hadn't been so disappointed in this book. Why was I so disappointed in this book? I finished it Saturday night and haven't really been able to gather the brain power to assign any words to this. Yesterday I read though some of the reviews and found many of the things that bothered me articulated much more clearly by other reviewers here:<br /><br />Badass Toby became lovesick high school girl Toby, whose entire identity seems to grow from her love interest. WTF feminist writer Margaret Atwood?<br /><br />Jimmy. Snowman. Snowman the Jimmy. Whatever you want to call him. We never even saw a glimmer of a return to his complex, fascinating character from Oryx and Crake in this book. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ All we get is a minute or two of weeping over Oryx's corpse, which is told through the perspective mostly of worrying about how the Crakers perceive this since they think Oryx is a god and can't possibly be a corpse on the ground. Which if you ask me, could have been a lot more compelling had this been the moment that the Crakers get a GLIMMER of an idea that their maker is not all-powerful and all-good and all-knowing. THAT would be a story I'd want to read.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The Crakers. With Jimmy comatose as the book opens, Toby takes over the duty of telling them the stories to explain their place in the world and the things that are happening in it. At first the way you hear these stories will be charming. You'll chuckle, even. You can just hear the unwritten childlike questions posed by the Crakers! Oh how funny that they sing every time Crake is mentioned! Oh my, now Toby has to eat that uncooked fish! But it wears a bit thin at the 50th installment. Atwood attempts to mine a bigger meaning out of this storyline with one of the Crakers eventually learning to communicate himself, through Toby teaching him writing and storytelling. Near as I can figure, this is about the biggest message she is trying to deliver through the entire series. Something about the importance of communication maybe? Or education? What?? When did this become an after school special?? <br /><br />And let's be honest. What was so compelling about Oryx and Crake, and Year of the Flood, was the way you could really believe every single terrifying thing that happens in this genetically modified universe. And, you met some very human characters trying to cope with this world despite all their very real and relatable flaws. See my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31998210" rel="nofollow noopener">review of Oryx and Crake</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77392694" rel="nofollow noopener">Year of the Flood</a> for more detail on why I love this. But in the end? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The fucking Pigoons are the BFF's of the humans and Crakers?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>Sorry, I'm just not buying that, Margaret. Too much. Not to mention the detail someone else here already mentioned-- come on. ALL of the characters knew each other in former lives? And they just so happen to be among the couple dozen humans who survived the plague? WHAT A COINCIDENCE.<br /><br />I think my greatest disappointment in this story was that the final conflict in this story did not center around the complex new values or morals that would surely need to evolve after such a harrowing chapter in human history. Nor the difficulties that these very real characters would surely face in having to articulate and shape these new value systems in this new world. No. Instead all we got was <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ our trusty heroes besting two horrible (and conveniently one-dimensional) rapists who thankfully didn't impregnate any of the myriad of knocked up characters and calling it sunny new day on planet Earth. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This is probably the least coherent review I've ever written here but this is what I think: don't read this book. I don't feel any less enthusiastic about Oryx and Crake or Year of the Flood but I'd like to just pretend this last one isn't a part of the series. I expected so much more.<br /><br />Did I read this voraciously? Yes. Every night. Did I enjoy it? Sure. I didn't hate it. Can I recommend it? No I cannot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15941202698903694478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_672699336" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/672699336" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/672699336?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781844087877" href="/book/show/20448875-maddaddam">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=miscellaneous-fiction">miscellaneous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=usa-and-canada">usa-and-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer672699336" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16514517169135770449"><b>Why?</b><br/><br/>I read this because I enjoy Atwood's varied writing, I like reading dystopian and speculative fiction, and the the preceding two books in this trilogy were excellent, in different ways.<br/><br/><b>#1 Oryx and Crake reviewed here 4*</b>: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324423'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br/><b>#2 Year of the Flood reviewed here 4*</b>: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/227625117'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br/><b>#3 MaddAddam only 2*</b>. <br/><br/>I read each within a year or so of publication and didn't know it was planned as a trilogy until I finished the second. The first work</span>
  <span id="freeText16514517169135770449" style="display:none"><b>Why?</b><br /><br />I read this because I enjoy Atwood's varied writing, I like reading dystopian and speculative fiction, and the the preceding two books in this trilogy were excellent, in different ways.<br /><br /><b>#1 Oryx and Crake reviewed here 4*</b>: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324423">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><b>#2 Year of the Flood reviewed here 4*</b>: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/227625117">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><b>#3 MaddAddam only 2*</b>. <br /><br />I read each within a year or so of publication and didn't know it was planned as a trilogy until I finished the second. The first worked fine as a standalone; the second was a parallel story time-wise, but structured and narrated in a pleasingly different way, shedding new light on the first. And this one...? I can't see the point of it, and I wonder when she decided on a trilogy. It's not that it's badly written or boring, but it doesn't add anything pertinent: just a bit of backstory and a smattering of predictable follow up. <br /><br />This novel continues the trilogy's recurring theme of unintended consequences of good intentions gone wrong. Maybe that applies to the book itself. <br /><br /><b>Plot</b><br /><br />I made lots of notes, but very little happens, so I won't include them here: if you've read the other two, you'll know most of the plot and characters (though it's recapped at the start - and throughout, really), and if you haven't, there's little point reading this, which just fills in a few gaps and joins a few threads. <br /><br />Contrived scenarios are created for characters to explain things to others (at such times, it feels more like an amateur news report, rather than a novel), and the language they use is often annoyingly banal. Atwood is better than this. <br /><br />In addition to the now-familiar plot points of bio-terrorism, GM, evil corporations, pollution, child exploitation, sex industry, underclass, hacking and gaming, life-and-death escapes, attempted murder, actual murder, survival, new religion, love, birth and death, there's a rich, powerful and corrupt PetrOleum-based church. <br /><br /><b>Narrative Structure</b><br /><br />As in O&amp;C, this alternates between two timelines: surviving in the "present" post-Chaos world versus events leading up to the "waterless flood". <br /><br />And as in Flood, this has conventional passages interspersed with short but regular quasi-religious ones. In this case, they're Toby telling creation-type stories to the Crakers, with shades of Joyce Grenfell (you don't read the Crakers' comments in these passages, but do read her slightly exasperated responses to their incessant, childlike questions).<br /><br /><b>Ideas</b><br /><br />There are some provocative ideas, but they were largely wasted:<br /><br />The Crakers were designed as near-perfect humans: peaceful, trusting, optimistic, questioning, musical, beautiful, immune to many diseases. The most profound question is to what extent they are human: it's a philosophical question in the story (hammered home by people explicitly discussing it), but also a plot point. It's left somewhat open-ended. <br /><br />"The world has been changed utterly... the familiar is long dead... everything he used to love has been swept away."<br />"Once there were too many people and not enough stuff; now it's the other way round." So, plenty of loo roll and linen; survival is more a matter of not getting killed by dangerous beasts or criminally violent painballers.<br /><br />Obviously, survival pushes the boundaries of what one's prepared to eat: former vegetarians dabble in meat, but no one can face eating animal hybrids bred to have human hair that looks like something from a shampoo advert. Even the pigoons (pig-based animals with some human brain components) have complex rules about cannibalism. <br /><br />However, survival isn't just about finding food right now: "It's hard to concentrate on the idea of a future. She's too immersed in the present... It's tempting to drift, as the Crakers seem to do... No long-term goals."<br /><br />Our own pseudo-reality TV is bad enough; popular TV shows before the disaster are, worryingly, almost believable: "Mixed Martial Arts Felony Fights, Nitee-Nite live-streamed suicides or HottTotts kiddy porn or Hedsoff real-time executions." How low can we go?<br /><br /><b>Power of Stories</b><br /><br />Belief in stories is at the core of the Crakers' optimistic existence. "What is 'belief' but a willingness to suspend the negatives?" That's why it's so important to them that Snowman-the-Jimmy keeps telling them creation myths about Crake who made them and Oryx who cares for the animals. When he can't do it, Toby has to take over.<br /><br />Atwood knows the importance of stories too, and can be a powerful storyteller, but in this book, I found that power lacking, except when she was writing Toby's tales to the Crakers. And then, she is fully conscious of it; it becomes rather meta. As Toby says, "<b>There's the story, then there's the real story, then there's the story of how the story came to be told. Then there's what you leave out of the story. Which is part of the story too</b>."<br /><br />Thus Toby weaves: initially to placate the Crakers, but she finds a sort of comfort in the ritual, as well as a little fun. When they overhear Snowman-the-Jimmy swear, she explains that Fuck is an invisible helper of Crake's who comes at times of need, when called. They subsequently call to him.<br /><br />Writing and reading takes things to another level. Toby teaches a young Craker, but wonders if it was a good idea to do so - where it will lead. Reading her old journals is like "a whisper from the past... The voice your ghost would have".<br /><br /><b>Humour</b><br /><br />There is humour here, which is just as well, and I suppose making that work in a dystopian setting, without turning into screwball comedy is quite an achievement. <br /><br />Atwood even address the humour in such a dark situation directly: "Why is war so much like a practical joke?... hiding behind bushes, leaping out". Quite a sobering thought.<br /><br />The slogans at HelthWyzer aren't bad:<br />"FOLLOW THE SAFETY RULES AND KEEP YOUR HEAD! AND ITS CONTENTS!"<br />"YOUR MEMORY IS OUR IP, SO WE'LL PROTECT IT FOR YOU!"<br /><br />And having "oleaginous" as a code word provided quite a challenge for a character to use it in a plausible sentence. <br /><br /><b>Other Quotes</b><br /><br />* "It gives her a look of addled reproach."<br /><br />* "City types with disposable emotions who liked to think they were saving things." (of those who participated in an eco-scam.)<br /><br />* He "continued to work on [eating] his conundrum of [an artificial] sausage."<br /><br />* "Laminated eyes. Hard and shiny."<br /><br />* "Slippery with longing, every pore avid, every capillary suffused, and thrashed around like newts in a puddle."<br /><br />* "Blood is thinner than money."<br /><br />* Cheap soap smells "like wading through a sea of dying lilies or a clutch of elderly churchwomen."<br /><br />* A bloke's first girlfriend was "beginning to emerge from the sex-induced coma created by him through the magic of his first-contact-with-aliens puppy-on-speed gonadal enthusiasm."!<br /><br />* "Does she want bad news about what she fears or good news she won't believe?<br /><br />* Plants reclaim the man-made world: "They'll have a building cracked like a nut in a few years, they'll reduce it to rubble in a decade. Then the earth swallows the pieces. Everything digests, and is digested."<br /><br />* Taking a mild hallucinogen, "She doesn't want all-out brain fractals, just... a crinkling of the window glass that separates the visible world from whatever lies behind it."<br /><br />* An unhappy couple: "There was a pot of boiling rage on a private stove behind their closed curtains."<br /><br />* The internet is "full of holes, all the better to trap you with". You have been warned.<br /><br />* To go unnoticed in the pleeblands, it's "best to seem up to no good, in non-specific ways."<br /><br />* "The smell of a pleasure factory in the off hours, so sad... that meant you got loved only if you paid."<br /><br /><br /><b>Trailer</b><br />Official trailer for this book:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2013/07/19/trailer-for-maddaddam-by-margaret-atwood/">http://bookriot.com/2013/07/19/traile...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16514517169135770449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
